   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Diesel engine</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div> 
    <div id="contentSub"></div> 
    <div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
      Jump to: 
     <a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 
     <a href="#p-search">search</a> 
    </div> 
    <div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr">
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/220px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/330px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg/440px-Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_generator_on_an_oil_tanker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Diesel generator on an oil tanker
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The <b>diesel engine</b> (also known as a <b>compression-ignition</b> or CI engine) is an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> in which <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">ignition</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">fuel</a> that has been injected into the <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a> is initiated by the high temperature which a gas achieves when greatly compressed (<a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_compression" title="Adiabatic compression" class="mw-redirect">adiabatic compression</a>). This contrasts with spark-ignition engines such as a <a href="/wiki/Petrol_engine" title="Petrol engine">petrol engine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> engine) or <a href="/wiki/Gas_engine" title="Gas engine">gas engine</a> (using a gaseous fuel as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Petrol" title="Petrol" class="mw-redirect">petrol</a>), which use a <a href="/wiki/Spark_plug" title="Spark plug">spark plug</a> to ignite an air-fuel mixture.</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/220px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/330px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Dieselmotor_vs.jpg/440px-Dieselmotor_vs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1338" data-file-height="1905"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Dieselmotor_vs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A Diesel engine built by 
        <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN AG</a> in 1906
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The diesel engine has the highest <a href="/wiki/Thermal_efficiency" title="Thermal efficiency">thermal efficiency</a> (<a href="/wiki/Engine_efficiency" title="Engine efficiency">engine efficiency</a>) of any practical <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion" title="Internal combustion" class="mw-redirect">internal</a> or <a href="/wiki/External_combustion" title="External combustion" class="mw-redirect">external combustion</a> engine due to its very high <a href="/wiki/Expansion_ratio" title="Expansion ratio">expansion ratio</a> and inherent <a href="/wiki/Lean_mixture" title="Lean mixture" class="mw-redirect">lean</a> burn which enables heat dissipation by the excess air. A small efficiency loss is also avoided compared to two-stroke non-direct-injection gasoline engines since unburnt fuel is not present at valve overlap and therefore no fuel goes directly from the intake/injection to the exhaust. Low-speed diesel engines (as used in ships and other applications where overall engine weight is relatively unimportant) can have a thermal efficiency that exceeds 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mhi_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhi-2">[2]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Diesel engines are manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke" title="Two-stroke" class="mw-redirect">two-stroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Four-stroke" title="Four-stroke" class="mw-redirect">four-stroke</a> versions. They were originally used as a more efficient replacement for stationary <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engines</a>. Since the 1910s they have been used in <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and ships. Use in locomotives, trucks, <a href="/wiki/Heavy_equipment" title="Heavy equipment">heavy equipment</a> and electricity generation plants followed later. In the 1930s, they slowly began to be used in a few <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile" class="mw-redirect">automobiles</a>. Since the 1970s, the use of diesel engines in larger on-road and <a href="/wiki/Off-road_vehicle" title="Off-road vehicle">off-road vehicles</a> in the USA increased. According to the British Society of Motor Manufacturing and Traders, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> average for diesel cars accounts for 50% of the total sold, including 70% in France and 38% in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The world's largest diesel engine is currently a <a href="/wiki/W%C3%A4rtsil%C3%A4-Sulzer_RTA96-C" title="Wärtsilä-Sulzer RTA96-C">Wärtsilä-Sulzer RTA96-C</a> Common Rail marine diesel, which produces a peak power output of 84.42&nbsp;MW (113,210&nbsp;hp) at 102&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WSRT96C_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSRT96C-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p> 
     <p></p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg/220px-Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg/330px-Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg/440px-Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2256" data-file-height="1504"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Hornsby_Akroyd_lamp_oil_engine_no.10171_of_1905_at_GDSF_08.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Hornsby-Akroyd oil engine
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1885, the <a href="/wiki/UK" title="UK" class="mw-redirect">English</a> inventor <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Akroyd_Stuart" title="Herbert Akroyd Stuart">Herbert Akroyd Stuart</a> began investigating the possibility of using <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">paraffin oil</a> (very similar to modern-day <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a>) for an engine, which unlike <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">petrol</a> would be difficult to vaporise in a carburettor as its <a href="/wiki/Volatility_(physics)" title="Volatility (physics)" class="mw-redirect">volatility</a> is not sufficient to allow this.<sup id="cite_ref-aoi_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aoi-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>His <a href="/wiki/Hot_bulb_engine" title="Hot bulb engine" class="mw-redirect">hot bulb engines</a>, first prototyped in 1886 and built from 1891 by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hornsby_%26_Sons" title="Richard Hornsby &amp; Sons">Richard Hornsby and Sons</a>, used a pressurised <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injection</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-rwallis_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwallis-8">[8]</a></sup> The Hornsby-Akroyd engine used a comparatively low compression ratio, so that the temperature of the air compressed in the combustion chamber at the end of the compression stroke was not high enough to initiate combustion. Combustion instead took place in a separated combustion chamber, the "vaporizer" or "hot bulb" mounted on the cylinder head, into which fuel was sprayed. Self-ignition occurred from contact between the fuel-air mixture and the hot walls of the vaporizer.<sup id="cite_ref-mcneil_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcneil-9">[9]</a></sup> As the engine's load increased, so did the temperature of the bulb, causing the ignition period to advance; to counteract pre-ignition, water was dripped into the air intake.<sup id="cite_ref-wrangham_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wrangham-10">[10]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The modern Diesel engine incorporates the features of direct (airless) injection and compression-ignition. Both ideas were patented by Akroyd Stuart and Charles Richard Binney in May 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-aoi_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aoi-6">[6]</a></sup> Another patent was taken out on 8 October 1890, detailing the working of a complete engine—essentially that of a diesel engine—where air and fuel are introduced separately. The difference between the Akroyd engine and the modern Diesel engine was the requirement to supply extra heat to the cylinder to start the engine from cold. By 1892, Akroyd Stuart had produced an updated version of the engine that no longer required the additional heat source, a year before Diesel's engine.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/220px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/330px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg/440px-Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="683" data-file-height="1024"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel%27s_Engine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Diesel's original 1897 engine on display at the Deutsches Museum in Munich, Germany
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1892, Akroyd Stuart patented a water-jacketed vaporiser to allow <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratios</a> to be increased. In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Barton_Transport" title="Barton Transport">Thomas Henry Barton</a> at Hornsbys built a working high-compression version for experimental purposes, whereby the vaporiser was replaced with a <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_head" title="Cylinder head">cylinder head</a>, therefore not relying on air being preheated, but by combustion through higher <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratios</a>. It ran for six hours—the first time automatic ignition was produced by compression alone.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This was five years before Rudolf Diesel built his well-known high-compression prototype engine in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> was, however, subsequently credited with the compression ignition engine innovation, despite Akroyd-Stuart’s engine being patented two years earlier. The higher compression and thermal efficiency is what distinguishes Diesel's patent, of 3,500 kilopascals (508 psi), from Ackroyd-Stuart's hot bulb compression ignition engine patent, of about 600 kilopascals (87 psi). Diesel improved his engine further, whereas Akroyd Stuart stopped development on his engine in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1892 Diesel received patents in Germany, Switzerland, the United Kingdom and the United States for "Method of and Apparatus for Converting Heat into Work".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> In 1893 he described a "slow-combustion engine" that first compressed air thereby raising its temperature above the igniting-point of the fuel, then gradually introducing fuel while letting the mixture expand "against resistance sufficiently to prevent an essential increase of temperature and pressure", then cutting off fuel and "expanding without transfer of heat".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1894 and 1895 he filed patents and addenda in various countries for his Diesel engine; the first patents were issued in Spain (No.&nbsp;16,654), France (No.&nbsp;243,531) and Belgium (No.&nbsp;113,139) in December 1894, and in Germany (No.&nbsp;86,633) in 1895 and the United States (No.&nbsp;608,845) in 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He operated his first successful engine in 1897.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>At <a href="/wiki/Augsburg" title="Augsburg">Augsburg</a>, on August 10, 1893, Rudolf Diesel's prime model, a single 10-foot (3.0&nbsp;m) iron cylinder with a <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a> at its base, ran on its own power for the first time. Diesel spent two more years making improvements and in 1896 demonstrated another model with a theoretical efficiency of 75%, in contrast to the 10% efficiency of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>. By 1898, Diesel had become a millionaire. His engines were used to power <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipelines</a>, electric and water plants, automobiles and trucks, and marine craft. They were soon to be used in mines, <a href="/wiki/Oil_field" title="Oil field">oil fields</a>, factories, and transoceanic shipping.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1886: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Akroyd_Stuart" title="Herbert Akroyd Stuart">Herbert Akroyd Stuart</a> builds a prototype <a href="/wiki/Hot_bulb_engine" title="Hot bulb engine" class="mw-redirect">hot bulb engine</a>.</li> 
      <li>1891: <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Akroyd_Stuart" title="Herbert Akroyd Stuart">Herbert Akroyd Stuart</a> patents an internal combustion engine that uses a "hot bulb" and pressurized <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">fuel injection</a>.</li> 
      <li>1892: February 23, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> obtained a patent (RP 67207) titled "<i>Arbeitsverfahren und Ausführungsart für Verbrennungsmaschinen</i>" (Working Methods and Techniques for Internal Combustion Engines).</li> 
      <li>1892: Akroyd Stuart builds his first working Diesel engine.</li> 
      <li>1893: Diesel's essay titled <i>Theory and Construction of a Rational Heat-engine to Replace the Steam Engine and Combustion Engines Known Today</i> appeared.</li> 
      <li>1893: August 10, Diesel built his first working prototype in Augsburg.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-17">[17]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1896 <a href="/wiki/Blackstone_%26_Co" title="Blackstone &amp; Co">Blackstone &amp; Co</a>, a Stamford <a href="/wiki/Farm_implement" title="Farm implement" class="mw-redirect">farm implement</a> they built lamp start oil engines.</li> 
      <li>1897: <a href="/wiki/Adolphus_Busch" title="Adolphus Busch">Adolphus Busch</a> licenses rights to the Diesel Engine for the USA and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-17">[17]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1898: Diesel licensed his engine to <a href="/wiki/Branobel" title="Branobel">Branobel</a>, a Russian oil company interested in an engine that could consume non-distilled oil. Branobel's engineers spent four years designing a ship-mounted engine.</li> 
      <li>1899: Diesel licensed his engine to builders <a href="/wiki/Krupp" title="Krupp">Krupp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer</a>, who quickly became major manufacturers.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1900s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1902: Until 1910 <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a> produced 82 copies of the stationary diesel engine.</li> 
      <li>1903: Two first diesel-powered ships were launched, both for river and canal operations: <i><a href="/wiki/La_Petite-Pierre" title="La Petite-Pierre">La Petite-Pierre</a></i> in France, powered by Dyckhoff-built diesels, and <i><a href="/wiki/Vandal_(tanker)" title="Vandal (tanker)">Vandal</a></i> tanker in Russia, powered by Swedish-built diesels with an electrical transmission.</li> 
      <li>1904: The French built the first diesel <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a>, the Z.</li> 
      <li>1905: Four diesel engine <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbochargers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intercooler" title="Intercooler">intercoolers</a> were manufactured by Büchl (CH), as well as a <a href="/wiki/Scroll-type_supercharger" title="Scroll-type supercharger">scroll-type supercharger</a> from Creux (F) company.</li> 
      <li>1908: <a href="/wiki/Prosper_L%27Orange" title="Prosper L'Orange">Prosper L'Orange</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">Deutz</a> developed a precisely controlled injection pump with a needle injection nozzle.</li> 
      <li>1909: The prechamber with a hemispherical combustion chamber was developed by Prosper L'Orange with <a href="/wiki/Benz_%26_Cie." title="Benz &amp; Cie." class="mw-redirect">Benz</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1910s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1910: The Norwegian research ship <i><a href="/wiki/Fram" title="Fram">Fram</a></i> was a sailing ship fitted with an auxiliary diesel engine, and was thus the first ocean-going ship with a diesel engine.<sup id="cite_ref-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl-18">[18]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1912: The Danish built the first ocean-going ship exclusively powered by a diesel engine, <a href="/wiki/MS_Selandia" title="MS Selandia">MS&nbsp;<i>Selandia</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vulcanus.oli.tudelft.nl-18">[18]</a></sup> The first <a href="/wiki/Locomotive" title="Locomotive">locomotive</a> with a diesel engine also appeared.</li> 
      <li>1913: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> submarines used <a href="/wiki/New_London_Ship_and_Engine_Company" title="New London Ship and Engine Company">NELSECO</a> units. <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a> died mysteriously when he crossed the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> on the <a href="/wiki/SS_Dresden_(1897)" title="SS Dresden (1897)">SS&nbsp;<i>Dresden</i></a>.</li> 
      <li>1914: German <a href="/wiki/U-boat" title="U-boat">U-boats</a> were powered by MAN diesels.</li> 
      <li>1919: Prosper L'Orange obtained a patent on a prechamber insert and made a needle injection nozzle. First diesel engine from <a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1920s</p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png/220px-8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png/330px-8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png/440px-8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png 2x" data-file-width="877" data-file-height="557"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:8_cylinder_Burmeister_%26_Wain_Diesel_engine_for_MS_Glenapp_1920.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> One of the eight-cylinder 3200 I.H.P. Harland and Wolff—Burmeister &amp; Wain Diesel engines installed in the motorship 
        <i>Glenapp</i>. This was the highest powered Diesel engine yet (1920) installed in a ship. Note man standing lower right for size comparison.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1921: Prosper L'Orange built a continuous variable output <a href="/wiki/Injection_pump" title="Injection pump">injection pump</a>.</li> 
      <li>1922: The first vehicle with a (pre-chamber) diesel engine was <a href="/wiki/Tractor" title="Tractor">Agricultural Tractor</a> Type 6 of the <a href="/wiki/Karl_Benz#Toward_Daimler-Benz_and_the_first_Mercedes-Benz_in_1926" title="Karl Benz">Benz Söhne</a> agricultural tractor OE Benz Sendling.</li> 
      <li>1923: The first <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a> with pre-chamber diesel engine made by <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benz_%26_Cie." title="Benz &amp; Cie." class="mw-redirect">Benz</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft" title="Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft">Daimler-Motoren-Gesellschaft</a> testing the first air-injection diesel-engined truck.</li> 
      <li>1924: The introduction on the truck market of the diesel engine by commercial truck manufacturers in the IAA. <a href="/wiki/Fairbanks-Morse" title="Fairbanks-Morse">Fairbanks-Morse</a> starts building diesel engines.</li> 
      <li>1927: First <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">truck</a> injection pump and injection nozzles of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a>. First passenger car prototype of <a href="/wiki/Stoewer" title="Stoewer">Stoewer</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1930s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1930s: <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar_Inc." title="Caterpillar Inc.">Caterpillar</a> started building diesels for their tractors.</li> 
      <li>1930: First US diesel-power passenger car (<a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a> powered Packard) built in Columbus, Indiana (USA).<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1930: <a href="/wiki/Beardmore_Tornado" title="Beardmore Tornado">Beardmore Tornado</a> diesel engines power the British airship <a href="/wiki/R101" title="R101">R101</a>.</li> 
      <li>1932: Introduction of the strongest diesel truck in the world by MAN with 160&nbsp;hp (120&nbsp;kW).</li> 
      <li>1933: First European <a href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile" class="mw-redirect">passenger cars</a> with diesel engines (<a href="/wiki/Citro%C3%ABn" title="Citroën">Citroën</a> Rosalie); Citroën used an engine of the English diesel pioneer Sir <a href="/wiki/Harry_Ricardo" title="Harry Ricardo">Harry Ricardo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The car did not go into production due to legal restrictions on the use of diesel engines.</li> 
      <li>1934: First <a href="/wiki/Turbo" title="Turbo" class="mw-redirect">turbo</a> diesel engine for a <a href="/wiki/Railway_train" title="Railway train" class="mw-redirect">railway train</a> by <a href="/wiki/Maybach" title="Maybach">Maybach</a>. First streamlined, stainless steel passenger train in the US, the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Zephyr" title="Pioneer Zephyr">Pioneer Zephyr</a>, using a Winton engine.</li> 
      <li>1934: First tank equipped with diesel engine, the Polish <a href="/wiki/7TP" title="7TP">7TP</a>.</li> 
      <li>1934–35: <a href="/wiki/Junkers_(Aircraft)" title="Junkers (Aircraft)" class="mw-redirect">Junkers Motorenwerke</a> in Germany started production of the Jumo aviation diesel engine family, the most famous of these being the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Jumo_205" title="Junkers Jumo 205">Jumo 205</a>, of which over 900 examples were produced by the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/220px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" width="220" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/330px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg/440px-Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1398" data-file-height="2118"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Lumbar_patent_dieselengine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Diesel" title="Rudolf Diesel">Rudolf Diesel</a>'s 1893 patent on his engine design
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1936: <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> built the <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_260_D" title="Mercedes-Benz 260 D">260D</a> diesel car.<sup id="cite_ref-MB260D_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MB260D-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atchison,_Topeka_and_Santa_Fe_Railway" title="Atchison, Topeka and Santa Fe Railway">AT&amp;SF</a> inaugurated the diesel train <a href="/wiki/Super_Chief" title="Super Chief">Super Chief</a>. The airship <a href="/wiki/Hindenburg_(airship)" title="Hindenburg (airship)" class="mw-redirect">Hindenburg</a> was powered by diesel engines. First series of passenger cars manufactured with diesel engine (<a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a> 260 D, <a href="/wiki/Hanomag" title="Hanomag">Hanomag</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saurer" title="Saurer">Saurer</a>). <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Benz" title="Daimler Benz" class="mw-redirect">Daimler Benz</a> airship diesel engine 602LOF6 for the LZ129 <i>Hindenburg</i> <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">airship</a>.</li> 
      <li>1937: The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Kharkiv_model_V-2" title="Kharkiv model V-2">Kharkiv model V-2</a> diesel engine, later used in the <a href="/wiki/T-34" title="T-34">T-34</a> tanks, widely regarded as the best tank chassis of World War II.</li> 
      <li>1937: <a href="/wiki/BMW_114" title="BMW 114">BMW 114</a> experimental airplane diesel engine development.</li> 
      <li>1938: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a> forms the GM Diesel Division, later to become <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a>, and introduces the <a href="/wiki/Series_71" title="Series 71" class="mw-redirect">Series 71</a> <a href="/wiki/Straight_engine" title="Straight engine">inline</a> high-speed medium-horsepower <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_diesel_engine" title="Two-stroke diesel engine">two stroke</a> engine; GM's EMD subsidiary introduces the <a href="/wiki/EMD_567" title="EMD 567">567</a> two stroke medium-speed high-horsepower engine for locomotive, ship and stationary applications; These GM and EMD engines utilize GM's patented <a href="/wiki/Unit_injector" title="Unit injector">Unit injector</a>.</li> 
      <li>1938: First turbo diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Saurer" title="Saurer">Saurer</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1940s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1942: <a href="/wiki/Tatra_(company)" title="Tatra (company)">Tatra</a> started production of <a href="/wiki/Tatra_111" title="Tatra 111">Tatra 111</a> with <a href="/wiki/Air_cooling" title="Air cooling">air-cooled</a> V12 diesel engine.</li> 
      <li>1943–46: The common-rail (CRD) system was invented (and patented by) <a href="/wiki/Clessie_Cummins" title="Clessie Cummins">Clessie Cummins</a><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1944: Development of <a href="/wiki/Air_cooling" title="Air cooling">air cooling</a> for diesel engines by <a href="/wiki/Deutz_AG" title="Deutz AG">Klöckner Humboldt Deutz AG</a> (KHD) for the production stage, and later also for Magirus Deutz.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1950s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1953: <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">Turbo</a>-diesel truck for <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes</a> in small series.</li> 
      <li>1954: Turbo-diesel truck in mass production by <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a>. First diesel engine with an overhead <a href="/wiki/Cam_shaft" title="Cam shaft" class="mw-redirect">cam shaft</a> of <a href="/wiki/Daimler_Benz" title="Daimler Benz" class="mw-redirect">Daimler Benz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1958 EMD introduces turbocharging for its 567 series of medium speed, high horsepower locomotive, stationary and marine engines. Every subsequent engine (645 and 710) would incorporate this turbocharger.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1960s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1960: The diesel drive displaced <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbines</a> and coal fired steam engines.</li> 
      <li>1962–65: A <a href="/wiki/Compression_release_engine_brake" title="Compression release engine brake">diesel compression braking system</a>, eventually to be manufactured by Jacobs (of drill chuck fame) and nicknamed the "Jake Brake", was invented and patented by Clessie Cummins.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1968: <a href="/wiki/Peugeot" title="Peugeot">Peugeot</a> introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_204" title="Peugeot 204">204</a> small cars with a transversally mounted diesel engine and <a href="/wiki/Front-wheel_drive" title="Front-wheel drive">front-wheel drive</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1970s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1973: <a href="/wiki/DAF_Trucks" title="DAF Trucks">DAF</a> produced an air-cooled diesel engine.</li> 
      <li>1976 February: Tested a diesel engine for the <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> Golf passenger car. The Cummins <a href="/wiki/Common_Rail" title="Common Rail" class="mw-redirect">Common Rail</a> injection system was further developed by the ETH Zurich from 1976 to 1992.</li> 
      <li>1978: Mercedes-Benz produced the first passenger car with a turbo-diesel engine (Mercedes-Benz 300 SD).<sup id="cite_ref-MB260D_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MB260D-21">[21]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile_Diesel_engine" title="Oldsmobile Diesel engine">Oldsmobile</a> introduced the first passenger car diesel engine produced by an American car company.</li> 
      <li>1979: <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_604" title="Peugeot 604">Peugeot 604</a>, the first turbo-diesel car to be sold in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1980s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1985: ATI Intercooler diesel engine from DAF. European Truck Common Rail system with the IFA truck type W50 introduced.</li> 
      <li>1986: <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> 524td, the world's first passenger car equipped with an <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Diesel_Control" title="Electronic Diesel Control">electronically controlled injection pump</a> (developed by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bosch_GmbH" title="Robert Bosch GmbH">Bosch</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The same year, the <a href="/wiki/Fiat_Croma" title="Fiat Croma">Fiat Croma</a> was the first passenger car in the world to have a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine#Direct_injection" title="Diesel engine">direct injection</a> (turbocharged) diesel engine.<sup id="cite_ref-fiat.com_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fiat.com-27">[27]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1987: Most powerful production truck with a 460&nbsp;hp (340&nbsp;kW) MAN diesel engine.</li> 
      <li>1989: <a href="/wiki/Audi_100" title="Audi 100">Audi 100</a>, the first passenger car in the world with a turbocharged direct injection and electronic control diesel engine.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>1990s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>1991: European emission standards <a href="/wiki/Euro_1" title="Euro 1" class="mw-redirect">Euro 1</a> met with the truck diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania</a>.</li> 
      <li>1993: Pump nozzle injection introduced in Volvo truck engines.</li> 
      <li>1994: Unit injector system by Bosch for diesel engines. Mercedes-Benz unveils the first automotive diesel engine with four valves per cylinder.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Medium speed high horsepower locomotive, ship and stationary diesel engines have utilized four valves per cylinder since at least 1938.</li> 
      <li>1995: First successful use of <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> in a production vehicle, by <a href="/wiki/Denso" title="Denso">Denso</a> in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Hino_Motors" title="Hino Motors">Hino</a> "Rising Ranger" truck.</li> 
      <li>1996: First diesel engine with direct injection and four valves per cylinder, used in the <a href="/wiki/Opel_Vectra" title="Opel Vectra">Opel Vectra</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1997: First <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> diesel engine in a passenger car, the <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Romeo_156" title="Alfa Romeo 156">Alfa Romeo 156</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autonews.com_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autonews.com-31">[31]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1998: BMW made history by winning the 24 Hour <a href="/wiki/N%C3%BCrburgring" title="Nürburgring">Nürburgring</a> race with the 320d, powered by a two-litre, four-cylinder diesel engine. The combination of high-performance with better fuel efficiency allowed the team to make fewer pit stops during the long endurance race. <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> introduces three and four-cylinder turbodiesel engines, with Bosch-developed electronically controlled <a href="/wiki/Unit_injector" title="Unit injector">unit injectors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Smart_(automobile)" title="Smart (automobile)">Smart</a> presented the first <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> three-cylinder diesel engine used in a passenger car (the <a href="/wiki/Smart_City_Coup%C3%A9" title="Smart City Coupé" class="mw-redirect">Smart City Coupé</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>1999: <a href="/wiki/Euro_3" title="Euro 3" class="mw-redirect">Euro 3</a> of Scania and the first <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> truck diesel engine of <a href="/wiki/Renault" title="Renault">Renault</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>2000s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>2002: A street-driven Dodge Dakota pickup with a 735 horsepower (548&nbsp;kW) diesel engine built at <a href="/wiki/Gale_banks_engineering" title="Gale banks engineering" class="mw-redirect">Gale banks engineering</a> hauls its own service trailer to the <a href="/wiki/Bonneville_Salt_Flats" title="Bonneville Salt Flats">Bonneville Salt Flats</a> and set an <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_Internationale_de_l%27Automobile" title="Fédération Internationale de l'Automobile">FIA</a> land speed record as the world's fastest pickup truck with a one-way run of 222&nbsp;mph (357&nbsp;km/h) and a two-way average of 217&nbsp;mph (349&nbsp;km/h).</li> 
      <li>2004: In Western Europe, the proportion of passenger cars with diesel engine exceeded 50%. Selective catalytic reduction (SCR) system in Mercedes, Euro 4 with EGR system and particle filters of <a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN</a>. <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectric" title="Piezoelectric" class="mw-redirect">Piezoelectric</a> injector technology by Bosch.</li> 
      <li>2006: <a href="/wiki/Audi_R10_TDI" title="Audi R10 TDI">Audi R10 TDI</a> won the <a href="/wiki/12_Hours_of_Sebring" title="12 Hours of Sebring">12 Hours of Sebring</a> and defeated all other engine concepts. The same car won the <a href="/wiki/2006_24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="2006 24 Hours of Le Mans">2006 24 Hours of Le Mans</a>. <a href="/wiki/Euro_5" title="Euro 5" class="mw-redirect">Euro 5</a> for all <a href="/wiki/Iveco" title="Iveco">Iveco</a> trucks. <a href="/wiki/JCB_Dieselmax" title="JCB Dieselmax">JCB Dieselmax</a> broke the FIA diesel land speed record from 1973, eventually setting the new record at over 350&nbsp;mph (563&nbsp;km/h).</li> 
      <li>2007: <a href="/wiki/Lombardini" title="Lombardini" class="mw-redirect">Lombardini</a> develops a new 440&nbsp;cc <a href="/wiki/Straight-twin_engine" title="Straight-twin engine">twin-cyinder</a> <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> diesel engine,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> which two years later sees application in automotive use, in the <a href="/wiki/Ligier" title="Ligier">Ligier</a> microcars.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> At the time, this engine was considered to be the smallest twin-cyinder engine with a common rail system.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>2008: <a href="/wiki/Subaru" title="Subaru">Subaru</a> introduced the first <a href="/wiki/Horizontally_opposed" title="Horizontally opposed" class="mw-redirect">horizontally opposed</a> diesel engine to be fitted to a passenger car. This is a Euro 5 compliant engine with an <a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas_recirculation" title="Exhaust gas recirculation">EGR</a> system. <a href="/wiki/SEAT" title="SEAT">SEAT</a> wins the drivers' title and the manufacturers' title in the <a href="/wiki/FIA_World_Touring_Car_Championship" title="FIA World Touring Car Championship" class="mw-redirect">FIA World Touring Car Championship</a> with the <a href="/wiki/SEAT_Le%C3%B3n" title="SEAT León">SEAT León TDI</a>. The achievements are repeated in the following season.</li> 
      <li>2009: <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> won the 2009 Dakar Rally held in Argentina and Chile. The first diesel to do so. <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Touareg#Dakar_Rally" title="Volkswagen Touareg">Race Touareg 2</a> models finished first and second. The same year, <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a> is claimed the world's strongest truck with their FH16 700. An inline 6-cylinder, 16&nbsp;L (976&nbsp;cu&nbsp;in) 700&nbsp;hp (522&nbsp;kW) diesel engine producing 3150 Nm (2323.32&nbsp;lb•ft) of torque and fully complying with Euro 5 emission standards.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <p>2010s</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>2010: <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Motors" title="Mitsubishi Motors">Mitsubishi</a> developed and started mass production of its <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_4N1_engine" title="Mitsubishi 4N1 engine">4N13</a> 1.8&nbsp;L DOHC I4, the world's first passenger car diesel engine that features a <a href="/wiki/Variable_valve_timing" title="Variable valve timing">variable valve timing</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Scania_AB" title="Scania AB">Scania AB</a>'s V8 had the highest torque and power ratings of any truck engine, 730&nbsp;hp (544&nbsp;kW) and 3,500&nbsp;N·m (2,581&nbsp;ft·lb).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>2011: <a href="/wiki/Piaggio" title="Piaggio">Piaggio</a> launches a <a href="/wiki/Straight-twin_engine" title="Straight-twin engine">twin-cyinder</a> turbodiesel engine, with <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> injection, on its new range of <a href="/wiki/Microvan" title="Microvan">microvans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li> 
     </ul> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_principle">Operating principle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Operating principle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:DieselCycle_PV.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/220px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/330px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/DieselCycle_PV.svg/440px-DieselCycle_PV.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="420"></a>

        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:DieselCycle_PV.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> p-V Diagram for the Ideal Diesel cycle. The cycle follows the numbers 1–4 in clockwise direction. The horizontal axis is Volume of the cylinder. In the diesel cycle the combustion occurs at almost constant pressure. On this diagram the work that is generated for each cycle corresponds to the area within the loop.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Model_Engine_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="1924"></a>
