      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/220px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/330px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg/440px-Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1994"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Model_Engine_B_Luc_Viatour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Diesel engine model, right side
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote">
      See also: 
      <a href="/wiki/Diesel_cycle" title="Diesel cycle">Diesel cycle</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_internal_combustion_engine" title="Reciprocating internal combustion engine" class="mw-redirect">Reciprocating internal combustion engine</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The diesel internal combustion engine differs from the gasoline powered Otto cycle by using highly compressed hot air to ignite the fuel rather than using a spark plug (<i>compression ignition</i> rather than <i>spark ignition</i>).</p> 
     <p>In the true diesel engine, only air is initially introduced into the combustion chamber. The air is then compressed with a compression ratio typically between 15:1 and 23:1. This high compression causes the temperature of the air to rise. At about the top of the compression stroke, fuel is injected directly into the compressed air in the combustion chamber. This may be into a (typically <a href="/wiki/Toroid" title="Toroid">toroidal</a>) void in the top of the piston or a <i>pre-chamber</i> depending upon the design of the engine. The fuel injector ensures that the fuel is broken down into small droplets, and that the fuel is distributed evenly. The heat of the compressed air vaporizes fuel from the surface of the droplets. The vapour is then ignited by the heat from the compressed air in the combustion chamber, the droplets continue to vaporise from their surfaces and burn, getting smaller, until all the fuel in the droplets has been burnt. Combustion occurs at a substantially constant pressure during the initial part of the power stroke. The start of vaporisation causes a delay before ignition and the characteristic diesel knocking sound as the vapour reaches ignition temperature and causes an abrupt increase in pressure above the piston (not shown on the P-V indicator diagram). When combustion is complete the combustion gases expand as the piston descends further; the high pressure in the cylinder drives the piston downward, supplying power to the crankshaft.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>As well as the high level of compression allowing combustion to take place without a separate ignition system, a high <a href="/wiki/Compression_ratio" title="Compression ratio">compression ratio</a> greatly increases the engine's efficiency. Increasing the compression ratio in a spark-ignition engine where fuel and air are mixed before entry to the cylinder is limited by the need to prevent damaging <a href="/wiki/Pre-ignition#Pre-ignition" title="Pre-ignition" class="mw-redirect">pre-ignition</a>. Since only air is compressed in a diesel engine, and fuel is not introduced into the cylinder until shortly before top dead centre (<a href="/wiki/Top_Dead_Center" title="Top Dead Center" class="mw-redirect">TDC</a>), premature detonation is not a problem and compression ratios are much higher.</p> 
     <p>The p–V diagam is a simplified and idealised representation of the events involved in a Diesel engine cycle, arranged to illustrate the similarity with a <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a>. Starting at 1, the piston is at bottom dead centre and both valves are closed at the start of the compression stroke; the cylinder contains air at atmospheric pressure. Between 1 and 2 the air is compressed adiabatically—that is without heat transfer to or from the environment—by the rising piston. (This is only approximately true since there will be some heat exchange with the cylinder walls.) During this compression, the volume is reduced, the pressure and temperature both rise. At or slightly before 2 (TDC) fuel is injected and burns in the compressed hot air. Chemical energy is released and this constitutes an injection of thermal energy (heat) into the compressed gas. Combustion and heating occur between 2 and 3. In this interval the pressure remains constant since the piston descends, and the volume increases; the temperature rises as a consequence of the energy of combustion. At 3 fuel injection and combustion are complete, and the cylinder contains gas at a higher temperature than at 2. Between 3 and 4 this hot gas expands, again approximately adiabatically. Work is done on the system to which the engine is connected. During this expansion phase the volume of the gas rises, and its temperature and pressure both fall. At 4 the exhaust valve opens, and the pressure falls abruptly to atmospheric (approximately). This is unresisted expansion and no useful work is done by it. Ideally the adiabatic expansion should continue, extending the line 3–4 to the right until the pressure falls to that of the surrounding air, but the loss of efficiency caused by this unresisted expansion is justified by the practical difficulties involved in recovering it (the engine would have to be much larger). After the opening of the exhaust valve, the exhaust stroke follows, but this (and the following induction stroke) are not shown on the diagram. If shown, they would be represented by a low-pressure loop at the bottom of the diagram. At 1 it is assumed that the exhaust and induction strokes have been completed, and the cylinder is again filled with air. The piston-cylinder system absorbs energy between 1 and 2—this is the work needed to compress the air in the cylinder, and is provided by mechanical kinetic energy stored in the flywheel of the engine. Work output is done by the piston-cylinder combination between 2 and 4. The difference between these two increments of work is the indicated work output per cycle, and is represented by the area enclosed by the p–V loop. The adiabatic expansion is in a higher pressure range than that of the compression because the gas in the cylinder is hotter during expansion than during compression. It is for this reason that the loop has a finite area, and the net output of work during a cycle is positive.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_fuel_injection_systems">Early fuel injection systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early fuel injection systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Diesel's original engine injected fuel with the assistance of compressed air, which atomized the fuel and forced it into the engine through a nozzle (a similar principle to an aerosol spray). The nozzle opening was closed by a pin valve lifted by the camshaft to initiate the fuel injection before top dead centre (<a href="/wiki/Top_Dead_Center" title="Top Dead Center" class="mw-redirect">TDC</a>). This is called an <b>air-blast injection</b>. Driving the three stage compressor used some power but the efficiency and net power output was more than any other combustion engine at that time.</p> 
     <p>Diesel engines in service today raise the fuel to extreme pressures by mechanical pumps and deliver it to the combustion chamber by pressure-activated injectors without compressed air. With direct injected diesels, injectors spray fuel through 4 to 12 small orifices in its nozzle. The early air injection diesels always had a superior combustion without the sharp increase in pressure during combustion. Research is now being performed and patents are being taken out to again use some form of air injection to reduce the nitrogen oxides and pollution, reverting to Diesel's original implementation with its superior combustion and possibly quieter operation. In all major aspects, the modern diesel engine holds true to Rudolf Diesel's original design, that of igniting fuel by compression at an extremely high pressure within the cylinder. With much higher pressures and high technology injectors, present-day diesel engines use the so-called solid injection system applied by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Akroyd_Stuart" title="Herbert Akroyd Stuart">Herbert Akroyd Stuart</a> for his <a href="/wiki/Hot_bulb_engine" title="Hot bulb engine" class="mw-redirect">hot bulb engine</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">indirect injection</a> engine could be considered the latest development of these low speed <i>hot bulb</i> ignition engines.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_delivery">Fuel delivery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Fuel delivery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Diesel engines are also produced with two significantly different injection locations. "Direct" and "Indirect". Indirect injected engines place the injector in a pre-combustion chamber in the head which due to thermal losses generally require a "glow plug" to start and very high compression ratio. Usually in the range of 21:1 to 23:1 ratio. Direct injected engines use a generally donut shaped combustion chamber void on the top of the piston. Thermal efficiency losses are significantly lower in DI engines which facilitates a much lower compression ratio generally between 14:1 and 20:1 but most DI engines are closer to 17:1. The direct injected process is significantly more internally violent and thus requires careful design, and more robust construction. The lower compression ratio also creates challenges for emissions due to partial burn. Turbocharging is particularly suited to DI engines since the low compression ratio facilitates meaningful forced induction, and the increase in airflow allows capturing additional fuel efficiency not only from more complete combustion, but also from lowering parasitic efficiency losses when properly operated, by widening both power and efficiency curves. The violent combustion process of direct injection also creates more noise, but modern designs using "split shot" injectors or similar multi shot processes have dramatically amended this issue by firing a small charge of fuel before the main delivery which pre-charges the combustion chamber for a less abrupt and in most cases slightly cleaner burn.</p> 
     <p>A vital component of all diesel engines is a mechanical or electronic <a href="/wiki/Governor_(device)" title="Governor (device)">governor</a> which regulates the idling speed and maximum speed of the engine by controlling the rate of fuel delivery. Unlike Otto-cycle engines, incoming air is not throttled and a diesel engine without a governor cannot have a stable idling speed and can easily overspeed, resulting in its destruction. Mechanically governed fuel injection systems are driven by the engine's <a href="/wiki/Gear_train" title="Gear train">gear train</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-43">[43]</a></sup> These systems use a combination of springs and weights to control fuel delivery relative to both load and speed.<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-43">[43]</a></sup> Modern electronically controlled diesel engines control fuel delivery by use of an electronic control module (ECM) or electronic control unit (<a href="/wiki/Electronic_control_unit" title="Electronic control unit">ECU</a>). The ECM/ECU receives an engine speed signal, as well as other operating parameters such as intake manifold pressure and fuel temperature, from a sensor and controls the amount of fuel and start of injection timing through <a href="/wiki/Actuator" title="Actuator">actuators</a> to maximise power and efficiency and minimise emissions. Controlling the timing of the start of injection of fuel into the cylinder is a key to minimizing emissions, and maximizing <a href="/wiki/Fuel_economy_in_automobiles" title="Fuel economy in automobiles">fuel economy</a> (efficiency), of the engine. The timing is measured in degrees of crank angle of the <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> before top dead centre. For example, if the ECM/ECU initiates fuel injection when the piston is 10° before <a href="/wiki/Top_Dead_Centre" title="Top Dead Centre" class="mw-redirect">TDC</a>, the start of injection, or timing, is said to be 10° <a href="/wiki/BTDC" title="BTDC" class="mw-redirect">BTDC</a>. Optimal timing will depend on the engine design as well as its speed and load, and is usually 4° BTDC in 1,350–6,000 HP, net, "medium speed" locomotive, marine and stationary diesel engines.</p> 
     <p>Advancing the start of injection (injecting before the piston reaches to its SOI-TDC) results in higher in-cylinder pressure and temperature, and higher efficiency, but also results in increased engine noise due to faster cylinder pressure rise and increased <a href="/wiki/Oxides_of_nitrogen" title="Oxides of nitrogen" class="mw-redirect">oxides of nitrogen</a> (NO<sub>x</sub>) formation due to higher combustion temperatures. Delaying start of injection causes incomplete combustion, reduced fuel efficiency and an increase in exhaust smoke, containing a considerable amount of particulate matter and unburned <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_advantages">Major advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Major advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Diesel engines have several advantages over other internal combustion engines:</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>They burn less fuel than a petrol engine performing the same work, due to the engine's higher temperature of combustion and greater expansion ratio.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1">[1]</a></sup> Gasoline engines are typically 30% efficient while diesel engines can convert over 45% of the fuel energy into mechanical energy<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a> for further explanation).</li> 
      <li>They have no high voltage electrical ignition system, resulting in high reliability and easy adaptation to damp environments. The absence of coils, spark plug wires, etc., also eliminates a source of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interference" title="Electromagnetic interference">radio frequency emissions</a> which can interfere with navigation and communication equipment, which is especially important in marine and aircraft applications, and for preventing interference with <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescopes</a>.</li> 
      <li>The longevity of a diesel engine is generally about twice that of a petrol engine<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (June 2013)">better&nbsp;source&nbsp;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> due to the increased strength of parts used. Diesel fuel has better lubrication properties than petrol as well. Indeed, in <a href="/wiki/Unit_injector" title="Unit injector">unit injectors</a>, the fuel is employed for three distinct purposes: injector lubrication, injector cooling and injection for combustion.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/220px-Soybeanbus.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/330px-Soybeanbus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Soybeanbus.jpg/440px-Soybeanbus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="322"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Soybeanbus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Bus powered by 
        <b>biodiesel</b>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <ul> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">Diesel fuel</a> is distilled directly from petroleum. Distillation yields some gasoline, but the yield would be inadequate without <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_reforming" title="Catalytic reforming">catalytic reforming</a>, which is a more costly process.</li> 
      <li>Diesel fuel is considered safer than petrol in many applications. Although diesel fuel will burn in open air using a <a href="/wiki/Candle_wick" title="Candle wick">wick</a>, it will not explode and does not release a large amount of flammable vapor. The low <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> of diesel is especially advantageous in marine applications, where the accumulation of explosive fuel-air mixtures is a particular hazard. For the same reason, diesel engines are immune to <a href="/wiki/Vapor_lock" title="Vapor lock">vapor lock</a>.</li> 
      <li>For any given partial load the <a href="/wiki/Fuel_efficiency" title="Fuel efficiency">fuel efficiency</a> (mass burned per energy produced) of a diesel engine remains nearly constant, as opposed to petrol and turbine engines which use proportionally more fuel with partial power outputs.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>They generate less waste heat in cooling and exhaust.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1">[1]</a></sup></li> 
      <li>Diesel engines can accept super- or turbo-charging pressure without any natural limit, constrained only by the strength of engine components. This is unlike petrol engines, which inevitably suffer detonation at higher pressure.</li> 
      <li>The carbon monoxide content of the exhaust is minimal.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li> 
      <li><a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a> is an easily synthesized, non-petroleum-based fuel (through <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a>) which can run directly in many diesel engines, while gasoline engines either need adaptation to run <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_fuel" title="Synthetic fuel">synthetic fuels</a> or else use them as an additive to gasoline (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> added to <a href="/wiki/Gasohol" title="Gasohol" class="mw-redirect">gasohol</a>).</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_and_electronic_injection">Mechanical and electronic injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mechanical and electronic injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Many configurations of fuel injection have been used over the course of the 20th century.</p> 
     <p>Most present-day diesel engines use a mechanical single plunger high-pressure <a href="/wiki/Fuel_pump" title="Fuel pump">fuel pump</a> driven by the engine crankshaft. For each engine cylinder, the corresponding plunger in the fuel pump measures out the correct amount of fuel and determines the timing of each injection. These engines use <a href="/wiki/Fuel_injection" title="Fuel injection">injectors</a> that are very precise spring-loaded valves that open and close at a specific fuel pressure. Separate high-pressure fuel lines connect the fuel pump with each cylinder. Fuel volume for each single combustion is controlled by a slanted <a href="/wiki/Groove_(engineering)" title="Groove (engineering)">groove</a> in the plunger which rotates only a few degrees releasing the pressure and is controlled by a mechanical governor, consisting of weights rotating at engine speed constrained by springs and a lever. The injectors are held open by the fuel pressure. On high-speed engines the plunger pumps are together in one unit.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The length of fuel lines from the pump to each injector is normally the same for each cylinder in order to obtain the same pressure delay.</p> 
     <p>A cheaper configuration on high-speed engines with fewer than six cylinders is to use an axial-piston distributor pump, consisting of one rotating pump plunger delivering fuel to a valve and line for each cylinder (functionally analogous to points and distributor cap on an <a href="/wiki/Otto_engine" title="Otto engine">Otto engine</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-buckman_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckman-43">[43]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Many modern systems have a single fuel pump which supplies fuel constantly at high pressure with a <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> (single fuel line common) to each injector. Each injector has a <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">solenoid</a> operated by an electronic control unit, resulting in more accurate control of injector opening times that depend on other control conditions, such as engine speed and loading, and providing better engine performance and fuel economy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Both mechanical and electronic injection systems can be used in either <a href="/wiki/Direct_fuel_injection" title="Direct fuel injection" class="mw-redirect">direct</a> or <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">indirect injection</a> configurations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">Two-stroke</a> diesel engines with mechanical injection pumps can be inadvertently run in reverse, albeit in a very inefficient manner, possibly damaging the engine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Large ship <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">two-stroke</a> diesels are designed to run in either direction, obviating the need for a gearbox.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_injection">Indirect injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Indirect injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/170px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" width="170" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/255px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg/340px-Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1069" data-file-height="1661"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_Indirect_Injection_Toyota_motor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Arrow indicates opening from pre-chamber
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Indirect_injection" title="Indirect injection">Indirect injection</a>
     </div> 
     <p>An indirect injection diesel engine delivers fuel into a chamber off the <a href="/wiki/Combustion_chamber" title="Combustion chamber">combustion chamber</a>, called a pre combustion chamber or ante-chamber, where combustion begins and then spreads into the main combustion chamber, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">turbulence</a> created in the chamber. This system allows for a smoother, quieter running engine, and because combustion is assisted by turbulence, <a href="/wiki/Injector" title="Injector">injector</a> pressures can be lower, about 100&nbsp;bar (10&nbsp;MPa; 1,500&nbsp;psi), using a single orifice tapered jet injector. Mechanical injection systems allowed high-speed running suitable for road vehicles (typically up to speeds of around <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7003400000000000000♠</span>4,000&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Rpm" title="Rpm" class="mw-redirect">rpm</a></span>). The pre-chamber had the disadvantage of increasing heat loss to the engine's cooling system, and restricting the combustion burn, which reduced the efficiency by 5–10%.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Indirect injection engines are cheaper to build and it is easier to produce smooth, quiet-running vehicles with a simple mechanical system. In road-going vehicles most prefer the greater efficiency and better controlled emission levels of direct injection. Indirect injection diesels can still be found in the many ATV diesel applications.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_injection">Direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Direct injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Direct_fuel_injection" title="Direct fuel injection" class="mw-redirect">Direct injection</a> diesel engines have injectors mounted at the top of the combustion chamber. The injectors are activated using one of two methods - hydraulic pressure from the fuel pump, or an electronic signal from an engine controller.</p> 
     <p>Hydraulic pressure activated injectors can produce harsh engine noise. Fuel consumption is about 15–20% lower than indirect injection diesels. The extra noise is generally not a problem for industrial uses of the engine, but for automotive usage, buyers have to decide whether or not the increased fuel efficiency would compensate for the extra noise. The noise level of modern <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">common rail</a> direct injection diesel engines with multiple injections per combustion cycle is however comparable to those of their gasoline counterparts.</p> 
     <p>Electronic control of the fuel injection transformed the direct injection engine by allowing much greater control over the combustion.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unit_direct_injection">Unit direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Unit direct injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Unit_Injector" title="Unit Injector" class="mw-redirect">Unit Injector</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Unit direct injection also injects fuel directly into the cylinder of the engine. In this system the injector and the pump are combined into one unit positioned over each cylinder controlled by the camshaft. Each cylinder has its own unit eliminating the high-pressure fuel lines, achieving a more consistent injection. This type of injection system, also developed by Bosch, is used by <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> AG in cars (where it is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Pumpe-D%C3%BCse" title="Pumpe-Düse" class="mw-redirect">Pumpe-Düse</a>-System</i>—literally <i>pump-nozzle system</i>) and by Mercedes-Benz ("PLD") and most major diesel engine manufacturers in large commercial engines (<a href="/wiki/MAN_SE" title="MAN SE">MAN SE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caterpillar_Inc." title="Caterpillar Inc.">CAT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cummins" title="Cummins">Cummins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volvo" title="Volvo">Volvo</a>). With recent advancements, the pump pressure has been raised to 2,400 bars (240&nbsp;MPa; 35,000&nbsp;psi),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> allowing injection parameters similar to common rail systems.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Common_rail_direct_injection">Common rail direct injection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Common rail direct injection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Common_rail" title="Common rail">Common rail</a>
     </div> 
     <p>In common rail systems, the separate pulsing high-pressure fuel line to each cylinder's injector is also eliminated. Instead, a high-pressure pump pressurizes fuel at up to 2,500&nbsp;bar (250&nbsp;MPa; 36,000&nbsp;psi),<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> in a "common rail". The common rail is a tube that supplies each computer-controlled injector containing a precision-machined nozzle and a plunger driven by a <a href="/wiki/Solenoid" title="Solenoid">solenoid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Piezoelectricity" title="Piezoelectricity">piezoelectric</a> actuator.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_weather">Cold weather</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cold weather">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Starting">Starting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Starting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>In cold weather, high speed diesel engines can be difficult to start because the mass of the cylinder block and cylinder head absorb the heat of compression, preventing ignition due to the higher surface-to-volume ratio. Pre-chambered engines make use of small electric heaters inside the pre-chambers called <a href="/wiki/Glowplug" title="Glowplug">glowplugs</a>, while direct-injected engines have these glowplugs in the combustion chamber.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p> 
     <p>Many engines use resistive heaters in the <a href="/wiki/Intake_manifold" title="Intake manifold" class="mw-redirect">intake manifold</a> to warm the inlet air for starting, or until the engine reaches <a href="/wiki/Operating_temperature" title="Operating temperature">operating temperature</a>. <a href="/wiki/Engine_block_heater" title="Engine block heater" class="mw-redirect">Engine block heaters</a> (electric resistive heaters in the engine block) connected to the utility grid are used in cold climates when an engine is turned off for extended periods (more than an hour), to reduce startup time and engine wear. Block heaters are also used for emergency power <a href="/wiki/Standby_generator" title="Standby generator">standby Diesel-powered generators</a> which must rapidly pick up load on a power failure. In the past, a wider variety of cold-start methods were used. Some engines, such as <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> engines used<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (September 2010)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> a system to introduce small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ether</a> into the inlet manifold to start combustion. Others used a mixed system, with a resistive heater burning <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>. An impromptu method, particularly on out-of-tune engines, is to manually spray an aerosol can of ether-based engine starter fluid into the intake air stream (usually through the intake air filter assembly).</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gelling">Gelling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Gelling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>Diesel fuel is also prone to <i>waxing</i> or <i>gelling</i> in cold weather; both are terms for the solidification of diesel oil into a partially crystalline state. The crystals build up in the fuel line (especially in fuel filters), eventually starving the engine of fuel and causing it to stop running. Low-output electric heaters in <a href="/wiki/Fuel_tank" title="Fuel tank">fuel tanks</a> and around fuel lines are used to solve this problem. Also, most engines have a <i>spill return</i> system, by which any excess fuel from the injector pump and injectors is returned to the fuel tank. Once the engine has warmed, returning warm fuel prevents waxing in the tank.</p> 
     <p>Due to improvements in fuel technology with additives, waxing rarely occurs in all but the coldest weather when a mix of diesel and <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> may be used to run a vehicle. Gas stations in regions with a cold climate are required to offer <a href="/wiki/Winter_diesel_fuel" title="Winter diesel fuel">winterized diesel</a> in the cold seasons that allow operation below a specific <a href="/wiki/Cold_filter_plugging_point" title="Cold filter plugging point">Cold Filter Plugging Point</a>. In Europe these diesel characteristics are described in the <a href="/wiki/EN_590" title="EN 590">EN 590</a> standard.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supercharging_and_turbocharging">Supercharging and turbocharging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Supercharging and turbocharging">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Most diesels are now <a href="/wiki/Turbocharging" title="Turbocharging" class="mw-redirect">turbocharged</a> and some are both turbo charged and <a href="/wiki/Supercharged" title="Supercharged" class="mw-redirect">supercharged</a>. Because diesels do not have fuel in the cylinder before combustion is initiated, more than one bar (100 kPa) of air can be loaded in the cylinder without preignition. A turbocharged engine can produce significantly more power than a naturally aspirated engine of the same configuration, as having more air in the cylinders allows more fuel to be burned and thus more power to be produced. A supercharger is powered mechanically by the engine's <a href="/wiki/Crankshaft" title="Crankshaft">crankshaft</a>, while a turbocharger is powered by the engine exhaust, not requiring any mechanical power. Turbocharging can improve the fuel economy<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> of diesel engines by recovering waste heat from the exhaust, increasing the excess air factor, and increasing the ratio of engine output to friction losses.</p> 
     <p><span id="Scavenge_blower"></span>A <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_engine" title="Two-stroke engine">two-stroke engine</a> does not have a discrete exhaust and intake stroke and thus is incapable of self-aspiration. Therefore, all two-stroke engines must be fitted with a <a href="/wiki/Scavenge_blower" title="Scavenge blower" class="mw-redirect">blower</a> to charge the cylinders with air and assist in dispersing exhaust gases, a process referred to as <a href="/wiki/Scavenging_(automotive)" title="Scavenging (automotive)">scavenging</a>. In some cases, the engine may also be fitted with a turbocharger, whose output is directed into the blower inlet.</p> 
     <p>A few designs employ a hybrid turbocharger (a turbo-compressor system) for scavenging and charging the cylinders, which device is mechanically driven at cranking and low speeds to act as a blower, but which acts as a true turbocharger at higher speeds and loads. A hybrid turbocharger can revert to compressor mode during commands for large increases in engine output power.</p> 
     <p>As turbocharged or supercharged engines produce more power for a given engine size as compared to naturally aspirated engines, attention must be paid to the mechanical design of components, lubrication, and cooling to handle the power. Pistons are usually cooled with lubrication oil sprayed on the bottom of the piston. Large engines may use water, sea water, or oil supplied through <a href="/wiki/Telescoping_(mechanics)" title="Telescoping (mechanics)">telescoping</a> pipes attached to the crosshead.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size_groups">Size groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Size groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_engine_(PSF).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/230px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png" width="230" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/345px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png/460px-Diesel_engine_%28PSF%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1633" data-file-height="2147"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Diesel_engine_(PSF).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Two Cycle Diesel engine with 
        <a href="/wiki/Roots_blower" title="Roots blower" class="mw-redirect">Roots blower</a>, typical of 
        <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a> and some 
        <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a> Engines
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>There are three size groups of Diesel engines<sup id="cite_ref-Brit2and4_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit2and4-60">[60]</a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Small—under 188&nbsp;kW (252&nbsp;hp) output</li> 
      <li>Medium</li> 
      <li>Large</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_types">Basic types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Basic types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>There are two basic types of Diesel Engines<sup id="cite_ref-Brit2and4_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brit2and4-60">[60]</a></sup></p> 
     <ul> 
      <li>Four stroke cycle</li> 
      <li>Two stroke cycle</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_engines">Early engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Early engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Rudolf Diesel based his engine on Nikolaus Otto's 1876 engine, with the goal of improving its efficiency. Diesel's engine concepts were set forth in patents in 1892 and 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> As such, diesel engines in the late 19th and early 20th centuries used the same basic layout and form as industrial steam engines, with long-bore cylinders, external valve gear, cross-head bearings and an open crankshaft connected to a large <a href="/wiki/Flywheel" title="Flywheel">flywheel</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement" class="mw-redirect"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2011)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Diesel_engine#Dubious" title="Talk:Diesel engine">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> Smaller engines would be built with vertical cylinders, while most medium- and large-sized industrial engines were built with horizontal cylinders, just as steam engines had been. Engines could be built with more than one cylinder in both cases. The largest early diesels resembled the triple-expansion steam <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_engine" title="Reciprocating engine">reciprocating engine</a>, being tens of feet high with vertical cylinders arranged in-line. These early engines ran at very slow speeds—partly due to the limitations of their air-blast injector equipment and partly so they would be compatible with the majority of industrial equipment designed for steam engines; maximum speeds of 100–300&nbsp;<a href="/wiki/Revolutions_per_minute" title="Revolutions per minute">rpm</a> were common. Engines were usually started by allowing compressed air into the cylinders to turn the engine, although smaller engines could be started by hand.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1897, when the first Diesel engine was completed <a href="/wiki/Adolphus_Busch" title="Adolphus Busch">Adolphus Busch</a> traveled to Cologne and negotiated exclusive right to produce the Diesel engine in the USA and Canada. In his examination of the engine, it was noted that the Diesel at that time operated at thermodynamic efficiencies of 32–35%, while a typical triple expansion steam engine would operate at about 18%.<sup id="cite_ref-Busch_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Busch-17">[17]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the early decades of the 20th century, when large diesel engines were first being used, the engines took a form similar to the compound steam engines common at the time, with the piston being connected to the connecting rod by a <a href="/wiki/Crosshead_bearing" title="Crosshead bearing" class="mw-redirect">crosshead bearing</a>. Following steam engine practice some manufacturers made double-acting two-stroke and four-stroke diesel engines to increase power output, with combustion taking place on both sides of the piston, with two sets of valve gear and fuel injection. While it produced large amounts of power and was very efficient, the double-acting diesel engine's main problem was producing a good seal where the piston rod passed through the bottom of the lower combustion chamber to the crosshead bearing, and no more were built. By the 1930s turbochargers were fitted to some engines. Crosshead bearings are still used to reduce the wear on the cylinders in large long-stroke main marine engines.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_high_and_medium-speed_engines">Modern high and medium-speed engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern high and medium-speed engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/220px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/330px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Yanmar_2GM20.JPG/440px-Yanmar_2GM20.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2038" data-file-height="2059"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Yanmar_2GM20.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> A 
        <a href="/wiki/Yanmar_2GM20" title="Yanmar 2GM20">Yanmar 2GM20</a> marine diesel engine, installed in a 
        <a href="/wiki/Sailboat" title="Sailboat">sailboat</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>As with petrol engines, there are two classes of diesel engines in current use: two-stroke and four-stroke. The four-stroke type is the "classic" version, tracing its lineage back to Rudolf Diesel's <a href="/wiki/Prototype" title="Prototype">prototype</a>. It is also the most commonly used form, being the preferred power source for many motor vehicles, especially buses and trucks. Much larger engines, such as used for <a href="/wiki/Diesel_locomotive" title="Diesel locomotive">railroad locomotion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marine_propulsion" title="Marine propulsion">marine propulsion</a>, are often two-stroke units, offering a more favourable <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight_ratio" title="Power-to-weight ratio">power-to-weight ratio</a>, as well as better fuel economy. The most powerful engines in the world are two-stroke diesels of mammoth dimensions.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_diesel_engine" title="Two-stroke diesel engine">Two-stroke diesel engine</a> operation is similar to that of petrol counterparts, except that fuel is not mixed with air before induction, and the crankcase does not take an active role in the cycle. The traditional two-stroke design relies upon a mechanically driven <a href="/wiki/Roots_blower" title="Roots blower" class="mw-redirect">positive displacement blower</a> to charge the cylinders with air before compression and ignition. The charging process also assists in expelling (scavenging) <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> gases remaining from the previous power stroke.</p> 
     <p>The <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetype</a> of the modern form of the two-stroke diesel is the (high-speed) <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel_Series_71" title="Detroit Diesel Series 71">Detroit Diesel Series 71</a> engine, designed by <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Kettering" title="Charles F. Kettering">Charles F. "Boss" Kettering</a> and his colleagues at <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors Corporation</a> in 1938, in which the blower pressurizes a chamber in the engine block that is often referred to as the "air box". The (very much larger medium-speed) <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a> engine<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> is used as the <a href="/wiki/Prime_mover_(locomotive)" title="Prime mover (locomotive)">prime mover</a> in EMD <a href="/wiki/Diesel-electric_locomotive" title="Diesel-electric locomotive" class="mw-redirect">diesel-electric locomotive</a>, marine and stationary applications, and was designed by the same team, and is built to the same principle. However, a significant improvement built into most later EMD engines is the mechanically assisted turbo-compressor, which provides charge air using mechanical assistance during starting (thereby obviating the necessity for <a href="/wiki/Roots_supercharger" title="Roots supercharger" class="mw-redirect">Roots-blown</a> scavenging), and provides charge air using an exhaust gas-driven turbine during normal operations—thereby providing true turbocharging and additionally increasing the engine's power output by at least fifty percent.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[a]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg/220px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg" width="220" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg/330px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg/440px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="853" data-file-height="704"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-182-9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Three English Electric 7SRL diesel-alternator sets being installed at the Saateni Power Station, 
        <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> 1955
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In a two-stroke diesel engine, as the cylinder's <a href="/wiki/Piston" title="Piston">piston</a> approaches the bottom dead centre exhaust ports or valves are opened relieving most of the excess pressure after which a passage between the air box and the cylinder is opened, permitting air flow into the cylinder.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup> The air flow blows the remaining combustion gases from the cylinder—this is the scavenging process. As the piston passes through bottom centre and starts upward, the passage is closed and compression commences,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> culminating in fuel injection and ignition.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Refer to <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke#Two-stroke_diesel_engine" title="Two-stroke" class="mw-redirect">two-stroke diesel engines</a> for more detailed coverage of aspiration types and supercharging of two-stroke diesel engines.</p> 
     <p>Normally, the number of cylinders are used in multiples of two, although any number of cylinders can be used as long as the load on the crankshaft is counterbalanced to prevent excessive <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">vibration</a>. The inline-six-cylinder design is the most prolific in light- to medium-duty engines, though small V8 and larger inline-four displacement engines are also common. Small-capacity engines (generally considered to be those below five litres in capacity) are generally four- or six-cylinder types, with the four-cylinder being the most common type found in automotive uses. Five-cylinder diesel engines have also been produced, being a compromise between the smooth running of the six-cylinder and the space-efficient dimensions of the four-cylinder. Diesel engines for smaller plant machinery, boats, tractors, generators and pumps may be four, three or two-cylinder types, with the single-cylinder diesel engine remaining for light stationary work. Direct reversible two-stroke marine diesels need at least three cylinders for reliable restarting forwards and reverse, while four-stroke diesels need at least six cylinders.</p> 
     <p>The desire to improve the diesel engine's <a href="/wiki/Power-to-weight" title="Power-to-weight" class="mw-redirect">power-to-weight</a> ratio produced several novel cylinder arrangements to extract more power from a given capacity. The uniflow <a href="/wiki/Opposed-piston_engine" title="Opposed-piston engine">opposed-piston engine</a> uses two pistons in one cylinder with the combustion cavity in the middle and gas in- and outlets at the ends. This makes a comparatively light, powerful, swiftly running and economic engine suitable for use in aviation. An example is the <a href="/wiki/Junkers_Jumo_204" title="Junkers Jumo 204">Junkers Jumo 204/205</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Napier_Deltic" title="Napier Deltic">Napier Deltic</a> engine, with three cylinders arranged in a triangular formation, each containing two opposed pistons, the whole engine having three crankshafts, is one of the better known.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_low-speed_engines">Modern low-speed engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Modern low-speed engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Low-speed diesel engines (as used in ships and other applications where overall engine weight is relatively unimportant) often have a thermal efficiency which exceeds 50%.<sup id="cite_ref-bmmWW_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bmmWW-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mhi_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mhi-2">[2]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gas_generator">Gas generator</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Gas generator">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Free-piston_engine" title="Free-piston engine">Free-piston engine</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Before 1950, <a href="/wiki/Sulzer_(manufacturer)" title="Sulzer (manufacturer)">Sulzer</a> started experimenting with two-stroke engines with boost pressures as high as 6 <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_(unit)" title="Atmosphere (unit)">atmospheres</a>, in which all the output power was taken from an exhaust <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a>. The two-stroke pistons directly drove air compressor pistons to make a positive displacement gas generator. Opposed pistons were connected by linkages instead of crankshafts. Several of these units could be connected to provide power gas to one large output turbine. The overall thermal efficiency was roughly twice that of a simple gas turbine.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> This system was derived from <a href="/wiki/Ra%C3%BAl_Pateras_Pescara" title="Raúl Pateras Pescara">Raúl Pateras Pescara</a>'s work on free-piston engines in the 1930s.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages_and_disadvantages_versus_spark-ignition_engines">Advantages and disadvantages versus spark-ignition engines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages versus spark-ignition engines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"> 
      <tbody>
       <tr> 
        <td class="mbox-image"> 
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="262" data-file-height="204"></a>
         </div> </td> 
        <td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Diesel_engine&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
